(spacecraft)</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the NASA crewed spacecraft. For other uses of the term, see <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Orion_(disambiguation)" title="Orion (disambiguation)">Orion (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width:25em;"><caption>Orion</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Orion_with_ATV_SM.jpg"><img alt="Orion with ATV SM.jpg" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="4267" decoding="async" height="169" (22,899&#160;lb) <br/> Service module: 15,461&#160;kg (34,086&#160;lb) <br/> Total: 25,848&#160;kg (56,985&#160;lb)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Crew capacity</th><td>2&#8211;6<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deep_Space_or_Bust_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Deep_Space_or_Bust-3">[3]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Dimensions</th><td>3.3&#160;&#215;&#160;5&#160;m (11&#160;&#215;&#160;16&#160;ft)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Volume</th><td>Pressurized: 19.56&#160;m<sup>3</sup> (691&#160;cu&#160;ft)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OrionQuickFacts_4-0"><a scope="row">Built</th><td>3</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Launched</th><td>1</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">First launch</th><td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exploration_Flight_Test_1" style="text-align:center"></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center">Related spacecraft</th></tr><tr><th scope="row">Derived from</th><td><a href="/wiki/Crew_Exploration_Vehicle" title="Crew Exploration Vehicle">Crew Exploration Vehicle</a> &#8226; <a href="/wiki/Automated_Transfer_Vehicle" title="Automated Transfer Vehicle">ATV</a></td></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Orion Multi-Purpose Crew Vehicle</b> (<b>Orion MPCV</b>) is an American-European interplanetary <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">spacecraft</a> intended to carry a crew of four<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OrionQuickFacts_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-OrionQuickFacts-4">[4]</a></sup> astronauts to destinations at or beyond <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a> (LEO). Currently under development by the National Aeronautics and Space Administration (<a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a> (ESA) for launch on the <a href="/wiki/Space_Launch_System" title="Space Launch System">Space Launch System</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-report_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-report-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Orion is intended to facilitate human exploration of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Moon" title="The Moon">the Moon</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a> and of <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> and to retrieve crew or supplies from the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> if needed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space.com_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-space.com-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Orion MPCV was announced by NASA on May 24, 2011, and is currently under development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space.com_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-space.com-8">[8]</a></sup> Its design is based on the Orion <a href="/wiki/Crew_Exploration_Vehicle" title="Crew Exploration Vehicle">Crew Exploration Vehicle</a> from the cancelled <a href="/wiki/Constellation_program" title="Constellation program">Constellation program</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AIAA_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-AIAA-9">[9]</a></sup> It has two main modules.  The Orion command module is being built by <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Martin" title="Lockheed Martin">Lockheed Martin</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Michoud_Assembly_Facility" title="Michoud Assembly Facility">Michoud Assembly Facility</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Orion_service_module" title="Orion service module">Orion service module</a>, provided by the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-osm_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-osm-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> is being built by <a href="/wiki/Airbus_Defence_and_Space" title="Airbus Defence and Space">Airbus Defence and Space</a>.\n</p><p>The MPCV\'s first test flight (uncrewed), known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exploration_Flight_Test_1" title="Exploration Flight Test 1">Exploration Flight Test 1</a> (EFT-1), was launched atop a <a href="/wiki/Delta_IV_Heavy" title="Delta IV Heavy">Delta IV Heavy</a> rocket on December 5, 2014, on a flight lasting 4 hours and 24 minutes, landing at its target in the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a> at 10:29 Central<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASAspaceflt-5150911_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASAspaceflt-5150911-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C4Wauto-8698790_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-C4Wauto-8698790-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C4Wauto-3525671_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-C4Wauto-3525671-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20141205-HF_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20141205-HF-16">[16]</a></sup> (delayed from the prior day due to technical and weather problems).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The first mission to carry astronauts is not expected to take place until 2023 at the earliest, although NASA officials have said that their staff is working toward an "aggressive internal goal" of 2021.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foust_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Foust-18">[18]</a></sup> However, a July 2016 <a href="/wiki/Government_Accountability_Office" title="Government Accountability Office">Government Accountability Office</a> report cast doubt on even the 2023 launch date, suggesting it may slip up to six months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> The report gave only a 40% confidence in the 2021 launch date, and suggested the aggressive goal may be counterproductive to the program.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orion_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Funding_history_and_planning">Funding history and planning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orion_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Funding history and planning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>For fiscal years 2006 through 2018, the Orion program had expended funding totaling $15,983 million in nominal dollars. This is equivalent to $18,138 million adjusting to 2018 dollars using the NASA New Start Inflation Indices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable floatright">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Fiscal year\n</th>\n<th>Funding<br/>(USD, millions)\n</th>\n<th>Line item name\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2006\n</td>\n<td>839.2\n</td>\n<td>CEV<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2007\n</td>\n<td>714.5\n</td>\n<td>CEV<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2013\n</td>\n<td>1,138\n</td>\n<td>Orion MPCV<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2014\n</td>\n<td>1,197\n</td>\n<td>Orion Program<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2016\n</td>\n<td>1,270\n</td>\n<td>Orion Program<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2017\n</td>\n<td>1,350.0\n</td>\n<td>Orion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2018\n</td>\n<td>1,350.0\n</td>\n<td>Orion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2006-2018\n</td>\n<td>Total $15,983\n</td>\n<td>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Excluded from the prior Orion costs are:\n</p>\n<ol><li>Costs "for production, operations, or sustainment of additional crew capsules, despite plans to use and possibly enhance this capsule after 2021"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Costs of the first service module and spare parts<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> to be provided by the European Space Agency for the test flight of Orion in 2020 (about $1 billion)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Costs to assemble, integrate, prepare and launch the Orion and its launcher (funded under the NASA Ground Operations Project,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> currently about $400M<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> per year)</li>\n<li>Costs of the launcher, the <a href="/wiki/Space_Launch_System" title="Space Launch System">SLS</a>, for the Orion spacecraft</li></ol>\n<p>For 2019 to 2023, NASA "notional"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> yearly budgets for Orion range from $1.1 to $1.2 billion. As of late 2015, the Orion program has a 70% confidence level for its &#8220;first Orion mission with astronauts by 2023&#8221; according to the Associate Administrator for NASA, Robert Lightfoot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foust_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Foust-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There are no NASA estimates for the Orion program recurring yearly costs once operational, for a certain flight rate per year, or for the resulting average costs per flight. Bill Hill, NASA manager of exploration systems development has indicated &#8220;My top number for Orion, SLS, and the ground systems that support it is $2 billion or less&#8221; (annually).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> NASA associate administrator William H. Gerstenmaier has indicated, &#8220;[per mission] costs must be derived from the data and are not directly available. This was done by design to lower NASA\'s expenditures.&#8221;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Orion_Crew_Exploration_Vehicle_.28CEV.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Orion_Crew_Exploration_Vehicle_(CEV)">Orion Crew Exploration Vehicle (CEV)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orion_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Orion Crew Exploration Vehicle (CEV)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Crew_Exploration_Vehicle" title="Crew Exploration Vehicle">Crew Exploration Vehicle</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Orion_spacecraft_launch_configuration_(2009_revision).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2664" data-file-width="2908" decoding="async" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Orion_spacecraft_launch_configuration_%282009_revision%29.jpg/220px-Orion_spacecraft_launch_configuration_%282009_revision%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Orion_spacecraft_launch_configuration_%282009_revision%29.jpg/330px-Orion_spacecraft_launch_configuration_%282009_revision%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Orion_spacecraft_launch_configuration_%282009_revision%29.jpg/440px-Orion_spacecraft_launch_configuration_%282009_revision%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Orion_spacecraft_launch_configuration_(2009_revision).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><br/>Orion CEV design as of 2009.</div></div></div>\n<p>On January 14, 2004, U.S. President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> announced the <a href="/wiki/Crew_Exploration_Vehicle" title="Crew Exploration Vehicle">Crew Exploration Vehicle</a> (CEV) as part of the <i><a href="/wiki/Vision_for_Space_Exploration" title="Vision for Space Exploration">Vision for Space Exploration</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The CEV was partly a reaction to the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster" title="Space Shuttle Columbia disaster">Space Shuttle <i>Columbia</i> accident</a>, the subsequent findings and report by the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Accident_Investigation_Board" title="Columbia Accident Investigation Board">Columbia Accident Investigation Board</a> (CAIB), and the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>\'s review of the American space program. The CEV effectively replaced the conceptual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orbital_Space_Plane" title="Orbital Space Plane">Orbital Space Plane</a> (OSP), which was proposed after the cancellation of the <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Martin_X-33" title="Lockheed Martin X-33">Lockheed Martin X-33</a> program to produce a replacement for the Space Shuttle. As the <i>Vision for Space Exploration</i> was developed into the <a href="/wiki/Constellation_program" title="Constellation program">Constellation program</a> under NASA administrator <a href="/wiki/Sean_O%27Keefe" title="Sean O\'Keefe">Sean O\'Keefe</a>, the Crew Exploration Vehicle was renamed the Orion Crew Exploration Vehicle, after the <a href="/wiki/Orion_(constellation)" title="Orion (constellation)">stellar constellation of the same name</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Constellation proposed using the Orion CEV in both crew and cargo variants to support the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> and as a crew vehicle for a return to the Moon. The Apollo-like design included a service module for life support and propulsion and the crew/command module was originally intended to land on solid ground on the US west coast using airbags, but later changed to ocean splashdown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasaspaceflight2007_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasaspaceflight2007-44">[44]</a></sup> The Orion CEV weighs about 23 tonnes, less than the 30 tonne <a href="/wiki/Apollo_command_and_service_module" title="Apollo command and service module">Apollo command and service module</a>. The crew module would weigh about 8.9 tonnes, greater than the equivalent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apollo_command_module" title="Apollo command module">Apollo command module</a> at 5.8 tonnes. With a diameter of 5 metres as opposed to 3.9 metres, the Orion CEV would provide 2.5 times greater volume as compared to the Apollo CM.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> The service module was originally planned to use liquid <a href="/wiki/Methane#Liquid_methane_rocket_fuel" title="Methane">methane</a> (LCH<sub>4</sub>) as its fuel, but switched to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypergolic" title="Hypergolic">hypergolic</a> propellants due to the infancy of oxygen/methane-powered rocket technologies and the goal of launching the Orion CEV by 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Orion CEV design consisted of two main parts: a conical crew module (CM) and a cylindrical service module (SM) holding the spacecraft\'s propulsion system and expendable supplies. Both were based substantially on the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_command_and_service_module" title="Apollo command and service module">Apollo command and service modules</a> flown between 1967 and 1975.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Orion CEV was to be launched on the <a href="/wiki/Ares_I" title="Ares I">Ares I</a> rocket to low Earth orbit, where it would rendezvous with the <a href="/wiki/Altair_(spacecraft)" title="Altair (spacecraft)">Altair lunar surface access module (LSAM)</a> launched on a heavy-lift <a href="/wiki/Ares_V" title="Ares V">Ares V</a> launch vehicle for lunar missions.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cancellation_of_Constellation_program">Cancellation of Constellation program</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orion_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Cancellation of Constellation program">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Orion_lunar_orbit_(Sept_2006).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Orion_lunar_orbit_%28Sept_2006%29.jpg/220px-Orion_lunar_orbit_%28Sept_2006%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Orion_lunar_orbit_%28Sept_2006%29.jpg/330px-Orion_lunar_orbit_%28Sept_2006%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Orion_lunar_orbit_%28Sept_2006%29.jpg/440px-Orion_lunar_orbit_%28Sept_2006%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Orion_lunar_orbit_(Sept_2006).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist\'s conception of the Orion spacecraft as then designed in lunar orbit.</div></div></div>\n<p>On May 7, 2009, the Obama administration enlisted the <a href="/wiki/Review_of_United_States_Human_Space_Flight_Plans_Committee" title="Review of United States Human Space Flight Plans Committee">Augustine Commission</a> to perform a full independent review of the ongoing NASA space exploration program.  The commission found the then current <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constellation_Program" title="Constellation Program">Constellation Program</a> to be woefully under-budgeted, behind schedule by four years or more in several essential components, with significant cost overruns, and unlikely to be capable of meeting any of its scheduled goals under its current budget.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>  As a consequence, the commission recommended a significant re-allocation of goals and resources.  As one of the many outcomes based on these recommendations, on October 11, 2010, the Constellation program was cancelled, ending development of the Altair, Ares I, and Ares V. The Orion Crew Exploration Vehicle survived the cancellation and was renamed the Multi-Purpose Crew Vehicle (MPCV), to be launched on the <a href="/wiki/Space_Launch_System" title="Space Launch System">Space Launch System</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Orion_Multi-Purpose_Crew_Vehicle_.28MPCV.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Orion_Multi-Purpose_Crew_Vehicle_(MPCV)">Orion Multi-Purpose Crew Vehicle (MPCV)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orion_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Orion Multi-Purpose Crew Vehicle (MPCV)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Through the program restructuring from Constellation to Post Constellation, the Orion development program moved from the development of three different versions of the Orion capsule, each for a different task,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> to the development of a single version capable of performing multiple tasks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OrionQuickFacts_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-OrionQuickFacts-4">[4]</a></sup>  On October 30, 2014, the somewhat redesigned Multi-Purpose spacecraft completed its first flight readiness review (FRR), allowing the vehicle to be integrated with the Delta IV rocket and readied for launch.  On December 5, 2014 it was successfully launched into space and retrieved at sea after splashdown on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exploration_Flight_Test_1" title="Exploration Flight Test 1">Exploration Flight Test 1</a> (EFT-1), marking NASA\'s re-entry into the business of designing and producing new crewed spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asteroid_Redirect_Mission">Asteroid Redirect Mission</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orion_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Asteroid Redirect Mission">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_Redirect_Mission" title="Asteroid Redirect Mission">Asteroid Redirect Mission</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Astronaut_preparing_to_take_samples_from_the_captured_asteroid.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2160" data-file-width="3840" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Astronaut_preparing_to_take_samples_from_the_captured_asteroid.png/220px-Astronaut_preparing_to_take_samples_from_the_captured_asteroid.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Astronaut_preparing_to_take_samples_from_the_captured_asteroid.png/330px-Astronaut_preparing_to_take_samples_from_the_captured_asteroid.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Astronaut_preparing_to_take_samples_from_the_captured_asteroid.png/440px-Astronaut_preparing_to_take_samples_from_the_captured_asteroid.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Astronaut_preparing_to_take_samples_from_the_captured_asteroid.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist\'s concept of an astronaut on an <a href="/wiki/Extravehicular_activity" title="Extravehicular activity">EVA</a> taking samples from a captured asteroid; Orion in the background.</div></div></div>\n<p>This mission would have placed an asteroid in <a href="/wiki/Lunar_orbit" title="Lunar orbit">lunar orbit</a>, rather than sending astronauts to an asteroid in <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">deep space</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NextSteps_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-NextSteps-55">[55]</a></sup> It was a part of the <a href="/wiki/2014_United_States_federal_budget" title="2014 United States federal budget">FY2014 budget request</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NextSteps_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-NextSteps-55">[55]</a></sup> Originally planned for 2017, then 2020,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-steps_2015_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-steps_2015-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Option-B_Boulder_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Option-B_Boulder-57">[57]</a></sup> and then for December 2021,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sn-20160303_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-sn-20160303-58">[58]</a></sup> the mission was given its notice of defunding in April 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-defunded_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-defunded-59">[59]</a></sup> The launch vehicle would have been either a <a href="/wiki/Delta_IV_Heavy" title="Delta IV Heavy">Delta IV Heavy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Space_Launch_System" title="Space Launch System">SLS</a> or <a href="/wiki/Falcon_Heavy" title="Falcon Heavy">Falcon Heavy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa201507_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa201507-60">[60]</a></sup> The boulder would have arrived in lunar orbit by late 2025,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Option-B_Boulder_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Option-B_Boulder-57">[57]</a></sup> where it was to be further analyzed both by robotic probes and by a future <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Manned_mission_to_an_asteroid&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Manned mission to an asteroid (page does not exist)">crewed mission</a> called ARCM (Asteroid Redirect Crewed Mission).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wall-2013_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-wall-2013-61">[61]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The development of advanced <a href="/wiki/Solar_electric_propulsion" title="Solar electric propulsion">solar electric propulsion</a> technology originally meant for this mission continues for its potential application on the proposed <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Orbital_Platform-Gateway" title="Lunar Orbital Platform-Gateway">Lunar Orbital Platform-Gateway</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-defunded_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-defunded-59">[59]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Design">Design</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orion_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Design">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><span class="mw-3d-wrapper" data-label="3D"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Orion_Spacecraft.stl"><img alt="Interactive 3D models of the spacecraft, with the spacecraft on the right in exploded view." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2880" data-file-width="5120" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Orion_Spacecraft.stl/220px-Orion_Spacecraft.stl.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Orion_Spacecraft.stl/330px-Orion_Spacecraft.stl.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Orion_Spacecraft.stl/440px-Orion_Spacecraft.stl.png 2x" width="220"/></a></span> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Orion_Spacecraft.stl" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Interactive 3D models of Orion, with the spacecraft fully integrated on the left and in exploded view on the right.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Orion MPCV takes basic design elements from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apollo_command_module" title="Apollo command module">Apollo command module</a> that took astronauts to the Moon, but its technology and capability are more advanced. It is designed to support long-duration deep space missions, with up to 21 days active crew time plus 6 months quiescent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C4WDefault-7291456_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-C4WDefault-7291456-62">[62]</a></sup> During the quiescent period crew life support would be provided by another module such as a <a href="/wiki/Deep_Space_Habitat" title="Deep Space Habitat">Deep Space Habitat</a>. The spacecraft\'s life support, propulsion, thermal protection, and avionics systems are designed to be upgradeable as new technologies become available.\n</p><p>The MPCV spacecraft includes both crew and service modules, and a spacecraft adaptor.\n</p><p>The MPCV\'s crew module is larger than Apollo\'s and can support more crew members for short or long-duration missions. The service module fuels and propels the spacecraft as well as storing oxygen and water for astronauts. The service module\'s structure is also being designed to provide locations to mount scientific experiments and cargo.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Crew_module_.28CM.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Crew_module_(CM)">Crew module (CM)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orion_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Crew module (CM)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MACES_in_Orion_mock-up.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3280" data-file-width="4928" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/MACES_in_Orion_mock-up.jpg/220px-MACES_in_Orion_mock-up.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/MACES_in_Orion_mock-up.jpg/330px-MACES_in_Orion_mock-up.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/MACES_in_Orion_mock-up.jpg/440px-MACES_in_Orion_mock-up.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MACES_in_Orion_mock-up.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Interior of the Orion mock-up in October 2014.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NASA%27s_Orion_Spacecraft_Parachutes_Tested_at_U.S._Army_Yuma_Proving_Ground.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4928" data-file-width="3280" decoding="async" height="331" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/NASA%27s_Orion_Spacecraft_Parachutes_Tested_at_U.S._Army_Yuma_Proving_Ground.jpg/220px-NASA%27s_Orion_Spacecraft_Parachutes_Tested_at_U.S._Army_Yuma_Proving_Ground.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/NASA%27s_Orion_Spacecraft_Parachutes_Tested_at_U.S._Army_Yuma_Proving_Ground.jpg/330px-NASA%27s_Orion_Spacecraft_Parachutes_Tested_at_U.S._Army_Yuma_Proving_Ground.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/NASA%27s_Orion_Spacecraft_Parachutes_Tested_at_U.S._Army_Yuma_Proving_Ground.jpg/440px-NASA%27s_Orion_Spacecraft_Parachutes_Tested_at_U.S._Army_Yuma_Proving_Ground.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NASA%27s_Orion_Spacecraft_Parachutes_Tested_at_U.S._Army_Yuma_Proving_Ground.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Testing of Orion\'s parachute system.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Orion crew module (CM) is the reusable transportation capsule that provides a habitat for the crew, provides storage for consumables and research instruments, and serves as the docking port for crew transfers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reusable_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reusable-63">[63]</a></sup> The crew module is the only part of the MPCV that returns to Earth after each mission and is a 57.5&#176; <a href="/wiki/Frustum" title="Frustum">frustum</a> shape, similar to that of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apollo_command_module" title="Apollo command module">Apollo command module</a>. As projected, the CM will be 5.02 meters (16&#160;ft 6&#160;in) in diameter and 3.3 meters (10&#160;ft 10&#160;in) in length,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> with a mass of about 8.5 metric tons (19,000&#160;lb). It was manufactured by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lockheed_Martin_Corporation" title="Lockheed Martin Corporation">Lockheed Martin Corporation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> It will have more than 50% more volume than the Apollo capsule, which had an interior volume of 5.9&#160;m<sup>3</sup> (210&#160;cu&#160;ft), and will carry four to six astronauts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> After extensive study, NASA has selected the <a href="/wiki/AVCOAT" title="AVCOAT">Avcoat</a> <a href="/wiki/Ablation" title="Ablation">ablator</a> system for the Orion crew module. Avcoat, which is composed of <a href="/wiki/Silica_fiber" title="Silica fiber">silica fibers</a> with a resin in a honeycomb made of <a href="/wiki/Fiberglass" title="Fiberglass">fiberglass</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phenolic_resin" title="Phenolic resin">phenolic resin</a>, was formerly used on the Apollo missions and on select areas of the space shuttle for early flights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Orion\'s CM will use advanced technologies, including:\n</p>\n<ul><li>"<a href="/wiki/Glass_cockpit" title="Glass cockpit">Glass cockpit</a>" digital control systems derived from those of the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_787_Dreamliner" title="Boeing 787 Dreamliner">Boeing 787 Dreamliner</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fi_20061006_orion_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-fi_20061006_orion-68">[68]</a></sup></li>\n<li>An "autodock" feature, like those of Russian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Progress_spacecraft" title="Progress spacecraft">Progress spacecraft</a> and the European <a href="/wiki/Automated_Transfer_Vehicle" title="Automated Transfer Vehicle">Automated Transfer Vehicle</a>, with provision for the flight crew to take over in an emergency. Prior American spacecraft (Gemini, Apollo, and Space Shuttle) have all needed manual piloting for docking.</li>\n<li>Improved waste-management facilities, with a miniature camping-style toilet and the unisex "relief tube" used on the space shuttle (whose system was based on that used on <a href="/wiki/Skylab" title="Skylab">Skylab</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> (based on the <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_(spacecraft)" title="Soyuz (spacecraft)">Soyuz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salyut_programme" title="Salyut programme">Salyut</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mir" title="Mir">Mir</a> systems). This eliminates the use of the much-hated plastic "Apollo bags" used by the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo</a> crews.</li>\n<li>A nitrogen/oxygen (<span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>/<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>) mixed <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> at either sea level (101.3&#160;<a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">kPa</a> or 14.69&#160;<a href="/wiki/Pounds_per_square_inch" title="Pounds per square inch">psi</a>) or reduced (55.2 to 70.3&#160;kPa or 8.01 to 10.20&#160;psi) pressure.</li>\n<li>Far more advanced computers than on prior crew vehicles.</li></ul>\n<p>The CM will be built of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aluminium-lithium_alloy" title="Aluminium-lithium alloy">aluminium-lithium alloy</a> used on the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_external_tank" title="Space Shuttle external tank">Space Shuttle external tank</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Delta_IV_rocket" title="Delta IV rocket">Delta IV</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atlas_V" title="Atlas V">Atlas V</a> rockets. The CM will be covered in the same <a href="/wiki/Nomex" title="Nomex">Nomex</a> felt-like thermal protection blankets used on parts on the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">shuttle</a> not subject to critical heating, such as the payload bay doors. The reusable recovery parachutes will be based on the parachutes used on both the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo</a> spacecraft and the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Solid_Rocket_Booster" title="Space Shuttle Solid Rocket Booster">Space Shuttle Solid Rocket Boosters</a>, and will also use <a href="/wiki/Nomex" title="Nomex">Nomex</a> cloth for construction. Water landings will be the exclusive means of recovery for the Orion CM.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasaspaceflight2007_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasaspaceflight2007-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>\n</p><p>To allow Orion to mate with other vehicles, it will be equipped with the <a href="/wiki/NASA_Docking_System" title="NASA Docking System">NASA Docking System</a>, which is somewhat similar to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/APAS-95" title="APAS-95">APAS-95</a> docking mechanism used on the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Shuttle</a> fleet. The spacecraft will employ a <a href="/wiki/Launch_escape_system" title="Launch escape system">Launch Escape System</a> (LES) like that used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mercury_program" title="Mercury program">Mercury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo</a>, along with an Apollo-derived "Boost Protective Cover" (made of <a href="/wiki/Fiberglass" title="Fiberglass">fiberglass</a>), to protect the Orion CM from <a href="/wiki/Aerodynamics" title="Aerodynamics">aerodynamic</a> and impact stresses during the first &#8203;<span class="frac nowrap">2<span class="visualhide">&#160;</span><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>2</sub></span> minutes of ascent. Its designers claim that the MPCV is designed to be 10 times safer during ascent and reentry than the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-decision_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-decision-70">[70]</a></sup> The CM is designed to be refurbished and reused. In addition, all of the Orion\'s component parts have been designed to be as generic as possible, so that between the craft\'s first test flight in 2014 and its projected Mars voyage in the 2030s, the spacecraft can be upgraded as new technologies become available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reusable_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Reusable-63">[63]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="ATV-based_European_service_module_.28ESM.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="ATV-based_European_service_module_(ESM)">ATV-based European service module (ESM)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orion_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: ATV-based European service module (ESM)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Orion_service_module" title="Orion service module">Orion service module</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Orion_Service_Module.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Orion_Service_Module.jpg/220px-Orion_Service_Module.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Orion_Service_Module.jpg/330px-Orion_Service_Module.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Orion_Service_Module.jpg/440px-Orion_Service_Module.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Orion_Service_Module.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist\'s concept of an Orion spacecraft including the ATV-derived service module with a propulsion stage attached at the back</div></div></div>\n<p>In May 2011 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ESA" title="ESA">ESA</a> director general announced a possible collaboration with <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> to work on a successor to the <a href="/wiki/Automated_Transfer_Vehicle" title="Automated Transfer Vehicle">ATV</a> (Automated Transfer Vehicle).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> On June 21, 2012, <a href="/wiki/Airbus_Defence_and_Space" title="Airbus Defence and Space">Airbus Defence and Space</a> announced that they had been awarded two separate studies, each worth &#8364;6.5 million, to evaluate the possibilities of using technology and experience gained from <a href="/wiki/Automated_Transfer_Vehicle" title="Automated Transfer Vehicle">ATV</a> and <a href="/wiki/Columbus_(ISS_module)" title="Columbus (ISS module)">Columbus</a> related work for future missions. The first looked into the possible construction of a service module which would be used in tandem with the Orion capsule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> The second examined the possible production of a versatile multi purpose orbital vehicle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On November 21, 2012, the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">ESA</a> decided to develop an <a href="/wiki/Automated_Transfer_Vehicle" title="Automated Transfer Vehicle">ATV</a>-derived service module for the Orion MPCV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> The service module is being manufactured by <a href="/wiki/Airbus_Defence_and_Space" title="Airbus Defence and Space">Airbus Defence and Space</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bremen" title="Bremen">Bremen</a>, Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> announced on January 16, 2013 that the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">ESA</a> service module will first fly on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exploration_Mission_1" title="Exploration Mission 1">Exploration Mission 1</a>, the debut launch of the <a href="/wiki/Space_Launch_System" title="Space Launch System">Space Launch System</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-osm_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-osm-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:22em; ;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303512">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" &#8220; ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" &#8221; ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>\n<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">ESA\'s contribution is going to be critical to the success of Orion\'s 2018 mission</div>\n<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">&#8212;NASA Orion Program manager<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-osm_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-osm-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</cite>\n</p>\n</div>\n<p>Testing of the European service module began in February 2016, at the <a href="/wiki/Space_Power_Facility" title="Space Power Facility">Space Power Facility</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 16 February 2017 a &#8364;200m contract was signed between <a href="/wiki/Airbus" title="Airbus">Airbus</a> and the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a> for the production of a second <a href="/wiki/Orion_service_module" title="Orion service module">European service module</a> for use on the first crewed Orion flight, called <a href="/wiki/Exploration_Mission-2" title="Exploration Mission-2">Exploration Mission-2</a> (EM-2).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Launch_Abort_System_.28LAS.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch_Abort_System_(LAS)">Launch Abort System (LAS)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orion_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Launch Abort System (LAS)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Orion_abort_modes" title="Orion abort modes">Orion abort modes</a></div>\n<p>In the event of an emergency on the <a href="/wiki/Launch_pad" title="Launch pad">launch pad</a> or during ascent, a <a href="/wiki/Launch_escape_system" title="Launch escape system">launch escape system</a> called the Launch Abort System (LAS) will separate the crew module from the launch vehicle using a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solid_rocket" title="Solid rocket">solid rocket</a>-powered launch abort motor (AM), which will produce more thrust (though for a much shorter duration) than the <a href="/wiki/Atlas_(rocket_family)" title="Atlas (rocket family)">Atlas 109-D</a> booster that launched astronaut <a href="/wiki/John_Glenn" title="John Glenn">John Glenn</a> into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mercury_6" title="Mercury 6">orbit</a> in 1962.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> There are two other propulsion systems in the LAS stack: the attitude control motor (ACM) and the jettison motor (JM). The ACM is a thruster system on the escape tower used to position and orient the capsule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> The jettison motor is a <a href="/wiki/Solid-propellant_rocket" title="Solid-propellant rocket">solid rocket system</a> used to separate the LAS from the crew capsule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> On July 10, 2007, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orbital_Sciences" title="Orbital Sciences">Orbital Sciences</a>, the prime contractor for the LAS, awarded <a href="/wiki/Alliant_Techsystems" title="Alliant Techsystems">Alliant Techsystems</a> (ATK) a $62.5 million sub-contract to, "design, develop, produce, test and deliver the launch abort motor." <a href="/wiki/Alliant_Techsystems" title="Alliant Techsystems">ATK</a>, which had the prime contract for the first stage of the <a href="/wiki/Ares_I" title="Ares I">Ares I</a> rocket, intended to use a "reverse flow" design for the motor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> On July 9, 2008, <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> announced that ATK had completed a <a href="/wiki/Rocket_engine_test_facility#Sea_level_tests" title="Rocket engine test facility">vertical test stand</a> at a facility in <a href="/wiki/Promontory,_Utah" title="Promontory, Utah">Promontory, Utah</a> to test launch abort motors for the Orion spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Another long-time space motor contractor, <a href="/wiki/Aerojet" title="Aerojet">Aerojet</a>, was awarded the jettison motor design and development contract for the LAS. As of September 2008, <a href="/wiki/Aerojet" title="Aerojet">Aerojet</a> has, along with team members <a href="/wiki/Orbital_Sciences_Corporation" title="Orbital Sciences Corporation">Orbital Sciences</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Martin" title="Lockheed Martin">Lockheed Martin</a> and <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>, successfully demonstrated two full-scale test firings of the jettison motor. This motor is important to every flight in that it functions to pull the LAS tower away from the vehicle after a successful launch. The motor also functions in the same manner for an abort scenario.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Existing_craft_mockups_and_testing">Existing craft mockups and testing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orion_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Existing craft mockups and testing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Space_Vehicle_Mockup_Facility" title="Space Vehicle Mockup Facility">Space Vehicle Mockup Facility</a> (SVMF) in <a href="/wiki/Johnson_Space_Center" title="Johnson Space Center">Johnson Space Center</a>, includes a full-scale Orion capsule mock-up for astronaut training.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="#Exploration_Flight_Test_1">Exploration Flight Test 1</a> (EFT-1) Orion (originally designated OFT-1), constructed at <a href="/wiki/Michoud_Assembly_Facility" title="Michoud Assembly Facility">Michoud Assembly Facility</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-84">[84]</a></sup> was delivered by Lockheed Martin to the Kennedy Space Center on July 2, 2012<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> and launched and recovered on December 5, 2014.</li>\n<li>The <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Boilerplate_Test_Article&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Boilerplate Test Article (page does not exist)">Boilerplate Test Article</a> (BTA) underwent splashdown testing at the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Hydro_Impact_Basin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Hydro Impact Basin (page does not exist)">Hydro Impact Basin</a> of NASA\'s <a href="/wiki/Langley_Research_Center" title="Langley Research Center">Langley Research Center</a>.  This same test article has been modified to support Orion Recovery Testing in the Stationary and Underway recovery tests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> The BTA contains over 150 sensors to gather data on its test drops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Testing of the 18,000 pound mockup ran from July 2011 to January 6, 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></li>\n<li>The Ground Test Article (GTA) stack, located at Lockheed Martin in Denver, is undergoing vibration testing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> It is made up by the Orion Ground Test Vehicle (GTV) combined with its Launch Abort System (LAS). Further testing will see the addition of service module simulator panels and Thermal Protection System (TPS) to the GTA stack.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-90">[90]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Orion_Drop_Test_on_Feb_29.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1800" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Orion_Drop_Test_on_Feb_29.jpg/220px-Orion_Drop_Test_on_Feb_29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Orion_Drop_Test_on_Feb_29.jpg/330px-Orion_Drop_Test_on_Feb_29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Orion_Drop_Test_on_Feb_29.jpg/440px-Orion_Drop_Test_on_Feb_29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Orion_Drop_Test_on_Feb_29.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Orion Drop Test Article during a test on February 29, 2012</div></div></div>\n<ul><li>The Drop Test Article (DTA), also known as the Drop Test Vehicle (DTV) is undergoing test drops at the US Army\'s <a href="/wiki/Yuma_Proving_Ground" title="Yuma Proving Ground">Yuma Proving Ground</a> in Arizona. The mock Orion parachute compartment is dropped from an altitude of 25,000 feet from a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C-130" title="C-130">C-130</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-90">[90]</a></sup> Testing began in 2007. Drogue chutes deploy around 20,000 and 15,000 feet. Testing of the <a href="/wiki/Reefing" title="Reefing">reefing</a> staged parachutes includes partial failure instances including partial opening and complete failure of one of the three main parachutes. With only two chutes deployed the DTA lands at 33 feet per second, the maximum touchdown speed for Orion\'s design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Other related test vehicles include the now-defunct Orion Parachute Test Vehicle (PTV) and its replacement the Generation II Parachute Test Vehicle (PTV2). The drop test program has had several failures in 2007, 2008, and 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> The new PTV was successfully tested February 29, 2012 deploying from a <a href="/wiki/Boeing_C-17_Globemaster_III" title="Boeing C-17 Globemaster III">C-17</a>. Ten drag chutes will drag the mockup\'s pallet from the aircraft for the drop at 25,000 feet. The landing parachute set of eight is known as the Capsule Parachute Assembly System (CPAS).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> The test examined air flow disturbance behind the mimicked full size vehicle and its effects on the parachute system. The PTV landed on the desert floor at 17&#160;mph (7.6&#160;m/s).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> A third test vehicle, the PCDTV3, was successfully tested in a drop on April 17, 2012. "The test examined how Orion\'s wake, the disturbance of the air flow behind the vehicle, would affect the performance of the parachute system."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_testing">Environmental testing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orion_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Environmental testing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>NASA performed environmental testing of Orion from 2007 to 2011 at the <a href="/wiki/Glenn_Research_Center" title="Glenn Research Center">Glenn Research Center</a> Plum Brook Station in <a href="/wiki/Sandusky,_Ohio" title="Sandusky, Ohio">Sandusky, Ohio</a>. The Center\'s <a href="/wiki/Space_Power_Facility" title="Space Power Facility">Space Power Facility</a> is the world\'s largest <a href="/wiki/Thermal_vacuum_chamber" title="Thermal vacuum chamber">thermal vacuum chamber</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Launch_abort_system_.28LAS.29_testing"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch_abort_system_(LAS)_testing">Launch abort system (LAS) testing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orion_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Launch abort system (LAS) testing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>ATK Aerospace successfully completed the first Orion Launch Abort System (LAS) test on November 20, 2008. The LAS motor could provide 500,000&#160;<a href="/wiki/Pound_(force)" title="Pound (force)">lbf</a> (2,200&#160;<a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">kN</a>) of thrust in case an emergency situation should arise on the launch pad or during the first 300,000 feet (91&#160;km) of the rocket\'s climb to orbit. The 2008 test firing of the LAS was the first time a motor with reverse flow propulsion technology of this scale had ever been tested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The text near this tag is dated. (February 2014)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>On March 2, 2009, a full size, full weight command module mockup (pathfinder) began its journey from the Langley Research Center to the White Sands Missile Range, New Mexico, for at-gantry launch vehicle assembly training and for LAS testing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> On May 10, 2010, NASA successfully executed the LAS PAD-Abort-1 test at White Sands New Mexico, launching a boilerplate (mock-up) Orion capsule to an altitude of approximately 6000 feet. The test used three solid-fuel rocket motors&#160;&#8211; a main thrust motor, an attitude control motor and the jettison motor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>\n</p><p><b>Future LAS test plans:</b> As of  April&#160;2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Orion_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, NASA planned to launch the Orion Multi Purpose Crew Vehicle Ascent Abort 2 test flight (AA&#8209;2) from the <a href="/wiki/Spaceport_Florida_Launch_Complex_46" title="Spaceport Florida Launch Complex 46">Spaceport Florida Launch Complex 46</a> in 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pa20140211_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-pa20140211-101">[101]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-launch_Orion_splashdown_recovery_testing">Pre-launch Orion splashdown recovery testing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orion_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Pre-launch Orion splashdown recovery testing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Before the first test flight and recovery of the Orion space vehicle at sea in December 2014, several preparatory vehicle recovery tests were performed. In 2009 during the Constellation phase of the program, the Post-landing Orion Recovery Test (PORT) was designed to determine and evaluate methods of crew rescue and what kind of motions the astronaut crew could expect after landing. This would include conditions outside the capsule for the recovery team. The evaluation process supported NASA\'s design of landing recovery operations including equipment, ship and crew needs.\n</p><p>The PORT Test used a full-scale <a href="/wiki/Boilerplate_(spaceflight)" title="Boilerplate (spaceflight)">boilerplate</a>  (mock-up) of NASA\'s Orion crew module and was tested in water under simulated and real weather conditions. Tests began March 23, 2009 with a Navy-built, 18,000-pound boilerplate when it was placed in a test pool at the Naval Surface Warfare Center\'s Carderock Division in West Bethesda, Md. Full sea testing ran April 6&#8211;30, 2009, at various locations off the coast of NASA\'s Kennedy Space Center with media coverage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Under the Orion program testing, Orion continued the "crawl, walk, run" approach used in PORT testing. The "crawl" phase was performed August 12&#8211;16, 2013 with the Stationary Recovery Test (SRT).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>  The Stationary Recovery Test demonstrated the recovery hardware and techniques that were to be employed for the recovery of the Orion crew module in the protected waters of <a href="/wiki/Naval_Station_Norfolk" title="Naval Station Norfolk">Naval Station Norfolk</a> utilizing the <a href="/wiki/USS_Arlington_(LPD-24)" title="USS Arlington (LPD-24)">USS <i>Arlington</i></a> as the recovery ship.  The USS <i>Arlington</i> is a <a href="/wiki/San_Antonio-class_amphibious_transport_dock" title="San Antonio-class amphibious transport dock">LPD 17 amphibious assault ship</a>.  The recovery of the Orion crew module will utilize unique features of the LPD 17 class ship to safely and economically recover the Orion crew module and eventually its astronaut crew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The "walk" and "run" phases were performed with the Underway Recovery Test (URT). Also utilizing the LPD 17 class ship, the URT were performed in more realistic sea conditions off the coast of California in early 2014 to prepare the US Navy / NASA team for recovering the Exploration Flight Test 1 (EFT-1) Orion crew module.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>  The URT tests completed the pre-launch test phase of the Orion recovery system.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration_Flight_Test_1">Exploration Flight Test 1</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orion_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Exploration Flight Test 1">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exploration_Flight_Test_1" title="Exploration Flight Test 1">Exploration Flight Test 1</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Delta_IV_Heavy_on_pad_with_Orion_EFT-1_(KSC-2014-4686).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Delta_IV_Heavy_on_pad_with_Orion_EFT-1_%28KSC-2014-4686%29.jpg/220px-Delta_IV_Heavy_on_pad_with_Orion_EFT-1_%28KSC-2014-4686%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Delta_IV_Heavy_on_pad_with_Orion_EFT-1_%28KSC-2014-4686%29.jpg/330px-Delta_IV_Heavy_on_pad_with_Orion_EFT-1_%28KSC-2014-4686%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Delta_IV_Heavy_on_pad_with_Orion_EFT-1_%28KSC-2014-4686%29.jpg/440px-Delta_IV_Heavy_on_pad_with_Orion_EFT-1_%28KSC-2014-4686%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Delta_IV_Heavy_on_pad_with_Orion_EFT-1_(KSC-2014-4686).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>EFT-1</div></div></div>\n<p>At 7:05 AM EST on December 5, 2014 the Orion capsule was launched atop a <a href="/wiki/Delta_IV_Heavy" title="Delta IV Heavy">Delta IV Heavy</a> rocket for its first test flight, and splashed down in the Pacific Ocean about 4.5 hours later. Although it was not crewed, the two-orbit flight was NASA\'s first launch of a human-rated vehicle since the retirement of the Space Shuttle fleet in 2011. Orion reached an altitude of 3,600&#160;mi (5,800&#160;km) and speeds of up to 20,000&#160;mph (8,900&#160;m/s) on a flight that tested Orion\'s heat shield, parachutes, jettisoning components, and on-board computers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Orion was recovered by <a href="/wiki/USS_Anchorage_(LPD-23)" title="USS Anchorage (LPD-23)">USS&#160;<i>Anchorage</i></a> and brought to San Diego, California for its return to Kennedy Space Center in Florida.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Liftoff_of_Orion.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Liftoff sequence and space entry of Orion on 5 December 2014</div></div></div>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orion_program_mission_schedule">Orion program mission schedule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orion_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Orion program mission schedule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Proposed_SLS_and_Orion_missions" title="Proposed SLS and Orion missions">Proposed SLS and Orion missions</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lunar_Orbital_Platform-Gateway.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2160" data-file-width="3840" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Lunar_Orbital_Platform-Gateway.jpg/220px-Lunar_Orbital_Platform-Gateway.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Lunar_Orbital_Platform-Gateway.jpg/330px-Lunar_Orbital_Platform-Gateway.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Lunar_Orbital_Platform-Gateway.jpg/440px-Lunar_Orbital_Platform-Gateway.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lunar_Orbital_Platform-Gateway.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist\'s concept of the <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Orbital_Platform-Gateway" title="Lunar Orbital Platform-Gateway">Lunar Orbital Platform-Gateway</a> orbiting the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>. The Orion MPCV is docked on the left.</div></div></div>\n<p>As of July 2018, the first flight of NASA\'s next-generation heavy-lift rocket, the <a href="/wiki/Space_Launch_System" title="Space Launch System">Space Launch System</a> (SLS), is scheduled for mid-2020 for an Orion lunar flyby mission called <a href="/wiki/Exploration_Mission-1" title="Exploration Mission-1">Exploration Mission-1</a> (EM-1), but it will not include a human crew. Although NASA has always planned for the first flight of the SLS to take place without a crew on board, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trump_administration" title="Trump administration">Trump administration</a>\'s transition team asked, in early 2017, for an internal evaluation of the possibility of making it a crewed flight. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_M._Lightfoot,_Jr." title="Robert M. Lightfoot, Jr.">Robert Lightfoot</a>, then NASA\'s acting administrator, said "based on the results of this internal <a href="/wiki/Evaluation" title="Evaluation">evaluation</a>, a crewed flight would be <i>technically feasible</i>, but the agency will proceed with its initial plan to make the rocket\'s first flight uncrewed." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Orbital_Platform-Gateway" title="Lunar Orbital Platform-Gateway">Lunar Orbital Platform-Gateway</a> (LOP-G) is a proposed space station in <a href="/wiki/Lunar_orbit" title="Lunar orbit">lunar orbit</a> intended to serve as an all-in-one solar-powered communications hub, science laboratory, short-term habitation module, and staging area for rovers and other robots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jackson20180911_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jackson20180911-107">[107]</a></sup> Various components of the Gateway would be launched on commercial launch vehicles and on the <a href="/wiki/Space_Launch_System" title="Space Launch System">Space Launch System</a> as Orion co-manifested payloads on the flights <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exploration_Mission_3" title="Exploration Mission 3">EM-3</a> through EM-9.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Insider20170401_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Insider20170401-108">[108]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable sortable">\n<caption>Orion missions\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Name\n</th>\n<th>Acronym\n</th>\n<th>Rocket\n</th>\n<th>Crew\n</th>\n<th>Launch date\n</th>\n<th>Status\n</th>\n<th>Duration\n</th>\n<th>Destination\n</th>\n<th style="width:30%">Purpose\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Ares_I-X" title="Ares I-X">Ares I-X</a>\n</td>\n<td class="table-na" data-sort-value="" style="background: #ececec; color: black; vertical-align: middle; text-align: center;">Delegated to commercial launcher<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>26-42 days<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsf-20170922_115-6"><a href="#cite_note-nsf-20170922-115">[115]</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>L<sub>2</sub> Southern NRHO\n</td>\n<td>First logistics module supply mission and delivery of the robotic arm to LOP-G.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsf-20170406_122-12"><a href="#cite_note-nsf-20170406-122">[122]</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mars_missions">Mars missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orion_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Mars missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Human_mission_to_Mars" title="Human mission to Mars">Human mission to Mars</a></div>\n<p>The Orion capsule is designed to support future missions to send astronauts to Mars, probably to take place in the 2030s. Since the Orion capsule provides only about 2.25&#160;m<sup>3</sup> (79&#160;cu&#160;ft) of living space per crew member,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Repo_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Repo-125">[125]</a></sup> the use of an additional <a href="/wiki/Deep_Space_Habitat" title="Deep Space Habitat">Deep Space Habitat</a> module will be needed for long duration missions. The habitat module will provide additional space and supplies, as well as facilitate spacecraft maintenance, mission communications, exercise, training, and personal recreation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-early_dsh_proposal_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-early_dsh_proposal-126">[126]</a></sup> Some plans for DSH modules would provide approximately 70.0&#160;m<sup>3</sup> (2,472&#160;cu&#160;ft) of living space per crew member,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-early_dsh_proposal_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-early_dsh_proposal-126">[126]</a></sup> though the DSH module is currently only in its early planning stages.  DSH sizes and configurations may vary slightly, depending on crew and mission needs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>  The mission is planned to launch in 2033.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Gallery">Gallery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orion_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Gallery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul class="gallery
